style="max-width:90vw;max-height:90vh;object-fit:contain;cursor:zoom-out"></div><div class="blog-container" style="display:flex;justify-content:flex-start;align-items:flex-start;overflow:visible;position:relative;flex-direction:column;gap:32px"><div class="toc-blog" style="display:flex;flex-direction:column;gap:24px;max-width:100%;position:relative;top:0"><div class="toc-blog__title" style="color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;font-weight:400;line-height:18px;text-align:left">TABLE OF CONTENTS</div><div class="toc-blog__body" style="display:flex;flex-direction:column;gap:12px"></div></div><div style="flex:1;max-width:100%;margin-right:0%"><div class="blog-post"><p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The ToneShell backdoor, frequently associated with <strong>Mustang Panda</strong> (also known as Stately Taurus and Earth Preta, among other monikers), has been consistently deployed against government organizations, mainly in Southeast and East Asia, for cyber espionage.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Recently, this malware has resurfaced, likely targeting attendees of the 2024 <strong>International Institute for Strategic Studies</strong> (IISS) Defence Summit in Prague.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This campaign illustrates how cyber espionage and international strategy often intertwine as nations seek to infiltrate sensitive security and defense discussions to gain a strategic edge amid global conflicts, from the Russia-Ukraine war to rising tensions in the South China Sea.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">While combing through files on Hatching Triage, one name stood out, prompting us to investigate further and share our findings in this article.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This blog post will explore our findings, including the malware's execution techniques, capabilities, and the <a href="https://hunt.io/glossary/command-and-control-server-c2" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">command and control (C2) infrastructure</a> that facilitates its operations.</p>
<h2 id="The_IISS_Defence_Summit_An_Attractive_Target_for_Cyber_Espionage" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:32px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">The IISS Defence Summit: An Attractive Target for Cyber Espionage</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The IISS Prague Defence Summit, scheduled for November 8-10, 2024, is a new event modeled after the successful Shangri-La and Manama Dialogues. The summit is poised to become a central forum for discussing defense and security within the Euro-Atlantic region.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Attendees include senior political leaders, defense ministers, policymakers, and industry executives from Europe, the United States, and allied nations. Discussions include defense capacity-building, strategic stability, and emerging threats.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This summit is a prime target for cyber espionage due to the participation of high-level officials discussing sensitive issues like military strategy, defense cooperation, and responses to geopolitical tensions. Accessing these discussions offers adversaries a strategic edge by exposing major global players' defense plans and policies.</p>
<h2 id="File_Discovery_In_Triage__ANYRUN" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:32px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">File Discovery In Triage &amp; ANY.RUN</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">During routine analysis on Hatching Triage, we discovered an executable file,  <strong>"IISS PRAGUE DEFENCE SUMMIT (8 â 10 November 2024 ).exe,â</strong> uploaded on 16 August.  Given its relevance to an upcoming high-profile event, we decided to investigate further.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_1.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_1.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 1: Hatching Triage Sandbox Analysis of suspicious EXE (Source/Link: <a href="https://tria.ge/240816-pn7j9sydlj/behavioral5" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">Triage</a>)</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">To further solidify our suspicions, a review of the PCAP containing network traffic confirmed the malware communicating with its C2 server using the familiar magic bytes 17 03 03.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">These bytes often appear in posts and reports as indicators of Toneshell and PubLoad activity.<br>
<!-- -->We found the same executable file on <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/afe95446-ef52-4acb-a4fb-a76b636e8b8b/" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">ANY.RUN</a>, where it exhibited similar TTPs.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_2.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_2.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 2: ANY.RUN analysis of the IISS-themed executable. (Source/Link: <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/afe95446-ef52-4acb-a4fb-a76b636e8b8b/" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">ANY.RUN</a>)</div>
<h2 id="Decoy_Document_Analysis" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:32px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Decoy Document Analysis</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Before diving into the malware itself, letâs first examine the decoy PDF used in this attack. Upon extracting the archive, the user is presented with two folders: <strong>Annex 1</strong> and <strong>Annex 2</strong>.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The first folder contains the executable file mentioned above, while the second, contains the document seen in Figure 3 titled âAnnex 2 - IISS PRAGUE DEFENCE SUMMIT (8 â 10 November 2024) - Copy.pdf.â</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_3.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_3.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 3: Document posing as an agenda for the upcoming IISS Defence Summit</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The PDF is an exact copy of a legitimate document available on the IISS official website, with only its name altered. This tactic is designed to reassure the target by displaying a genuine agenda for the summit, reducing suspicion while the malware silently operates in the background.</p>
<h2 id="Uncovering_Malware_Behavior_and_Execution" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:32px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Uncovering Malware Behavior and Execution</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">As previously mentioned, the extracted ZIP file reveals two folders. Weâll now turn our attention to the suspicious file that caught our eye.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_4.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_4.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 4: Annex 1 &amp; 2 folders after extracting the zip contents</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Inside the Annex 1 folder (Figure 5), we see a file name matching that of what we found in Triage. For the keen-eyed, you may have noticed the file type is "Shortcut to MS-DOS Program," which suggests it is a program information file (PIF).</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_5.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_5.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 5: PIF-file masquerading as IISS agenda file</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">PIF files are shortcuts designed to provide metadata like a config file for MS-DOS programs. However, threat actors can use them as an alternative to .exe files to execute malicious code.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The PIF file acts as a dropper, which weâll soon see, and is signed by the â<strong>Hefei Nora Network Technology Co.</strong>â  A screenshot of the code signing certificate is below.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_6.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_6.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 6: Codesigning certificate used for the malicious PIF-file</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Analyzing the file in VirusTotal reveals the PIF-file has two aliases: <strong>fhbemb.exe</strong> and <strong>SFFWallpaperCore.exe</strong>.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This file also contains a PDB path of:</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><strong>G:\CLIENT\fhbemb\src\bin\Release_NL\fhbemb.pdb</strong></p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">In our research, we were unable to locate information suggesting either of the above file names (fhbemb.exe and SFFWallpaperCore.exe) are legitimate Windows programs.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">An April 2024 blog post by <a href="https://www-secrss-com.translate.goog/articles/64948?_x_tr_sl=auto&amp;_x_tr_tl=en&amp;_x_tr_hl=ja" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">secrss</a> uncovered a suspected <strong>APT-Q-27</strong> (aka Golden Eye Dog, Dragon Breath) operation that also used âfhbemb.exeâ to side load âlibemb.dllâ to execute a modified version of Gh0st RAT.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><a href="https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2023/05/03/doubled-dll-sideloading-dragon-breath/" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">Sophos</a> has also previously reported similar DLL sideloading techniques by this group.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Figure 7 illustrates the malware execution flow as detailed in the Secrss post.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_7.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_7.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 7: Secrss attack process diagram using similarly named files (Source: <a href="https://www-secrss-com.translate.goog/articles/64948?_x_tr_sl=auto&amp;_x_tr_tl=en&amp;_x_tr_hl=ja" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">Secrss</a>)</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Returning to the malicious PIF, upon execution, it checks for the presence of the FFWallpaperCore directory in C:\ProgramData. If the directory is absent, it drops SFFWallpaperCore.exe and libemb.dll, likely to verify whether the system has already been compromised.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Persistence is established by adding a registry run key and creating a scheduled task.</p>
<ul style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;margin:21px 0;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1.2em">
<li style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1em">Registry run key:</li>
</ul>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><strong>cmd.exe /C schtasks /F /Create /TN FFWallpaperEmbCore /SC minute /MO 6 /TR "C:\ProgramData\FFWallpaperCore\SFFWallpaperCore.exe FFWallpaper"</strong></p>
<ul style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;margin:21px 0;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1.2em">
<li style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1em">Creation of scheduled task</li>
</ul>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><strong>schtasks /F /Create /TN FFWallpaperEmbCore /SC minute /MO 6 /TR "C:\ProgramData\FFWallpaperCore\SFFWallpaperCore.exe FFWallpaper"</strong></p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The overall execution flow (<strong>Figure 8</strong>) follows a rather standard pattern commonly seen in malware operations.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_8.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_8.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 8: PIF event flow (Created using Lucidchart)</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">libemb.dll, written in C++, is signed by the same company as the EXE, but, as shown in Figure 9, the certificate is not trusted.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_9.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_9.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 9: Untrusted codesigning certificate for libemb.dll</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The DLL contains unique debug strings, which have become a hallmark of Mustang Panda malware. Within the file, we found two references to Twitter/X accounts: @Rainmaker1973 and @techyteachme, the latter belonging to Zack Allen, who also runs a great Detection Engineering newsletter if youâre interested.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_10.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_10.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 10: Unique strings including the X account name for Zack Allen. Also notice the string before âbuitengebieden,â which is Dutch for âoutlying areas.â</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">&nbsp;</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_11.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_11.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 11: Debug strings for X user Rainmaker1973</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">A network connection is established with the C2 server at 103.27.108.]14 on port 443. The traffic uses raw TCP but mimics TLS to evade detection.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This approach has been observed in multiple reports on Mustang Panda activity, specifically linked to ToneShell and Pubload malware.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Below is a PCAP screenshot from the initial communication with the C2 server.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_12.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_12.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 12: Request header containing the magic bytes â17 03 03â</div>
<h2 id="Network_Infrastructure" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:32px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Network Infrastructure</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The command and control server is hosted on Topway Global Limitedâs ASN in Hong Kong, with ports <strong>80</strong>, <strong>443</strong>, and <strong>3389</strong> accessible. Interestingly, the IP briefly presented a self-signed RDP certificate at the start of August, carrying the common name â<strong>WIN-USLKI5BA743</strong>.â</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Using RDP certificates has been a reliable method for tracking Mustang Pandaâs infrastructure in the past, but recent variations suggest the threat actors are aware of this detection technique and are adjusting accordingly.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This particular certificate was issued on <strong>Wednesday, August 25, 2021, at 03:36:30</strong>âa detail that may prove significant in our investigation.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Below is a screenshot from Hunt showing this certificate, along with historical TLS data, to aid in identifying related activity.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_13.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_13.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 13: SSL History data in Hunt showing the short-lived RDP certificate</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">With no additional domains or certificates to pivot on, we turn to Hunt's Advanced Search feature to identify servers using the same certificate, focusing specifically on the 'Not Before' date and time.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">By applying the query shown in <strong>Figure 14</strong>, we narrowed the results to just seven serversâsuggesting a potential link to the associated infrastructure. Notably, three of these servers were first observed only a few days ago, indicating recent and potentially active use at the time of writing.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_14.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_14.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 14: Results of the search for servers hosting RDP certificates bearing the same not before date</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">IPs sharing the same certificate:</p>

<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">As shown in the table above, nearly all the IP addresses reside on the same ASN as the C2 server, with one exception. Additionally, the proximity of these IPs to each other strengthens our assessment that these servers may be controlled by the same threat actor or group and hosted within a similar or adjacent range to maintain operational control and flexibility.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Notably, the C2 IP has not yet been flagged as malicious by any vendors on VirusTotal.</p>
<h2 id="Final_Thoughts" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:32px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Final Thoughts</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">While sandbox runs and dynamic analysis of the malware did not reveal the specific objectives of the threat actors once they gained access to infected systems, we can hypothesize that targeting a defense summit suggests an intent to gather intelligence on sensitive discussions.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">To mitigate such threats, Hunt recommends conducting regular phishing awareness exercises for all users, closely verifying email senders and domain names before downloading files, and deploying an endpoint detection and response solution to identify malicious execution patterns.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">If youâd like to stay ahead of threats like those uncovered in this post, <a href="https://hunt.io/demo?utm_campaign=huntio-blog-bottom-demo-cta&amp;utm_source=hunt.io&amp;utm_medium=website&amp;utm_content=huntio-top-heading-demo-button&amp;utm_term=huntio-top-heading-demo-button" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">request a demo</a> today to see how our tools can enhance your defenses.</p>

class="blog-container" style="display:flex;justify-content:flex-start;align-items:flex-start;overflow:visible;position:relative;flex-direction:column;gap:32px"><div class="toc-blog" style="display:flex;flex-direction:column;gap:24px;max-width:100%;position:relative;top:0"><div class="toc-blog__title" style="color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;font-weight:400;line-height:18px;text-align:left">TABLE OF CONTENTS</div><div class="toc-blog__body" style="display:flex;flex-direction:column;gap:12px"></div></div><div style="flex:1;max-width:100%;margin-right:0%"><div class="blog-post"><p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The ToneShell backdoor, frequently associated with <strong>Mustang Panda</strong> (also known as Stately Taurus and Earth Preta, among other monikers), has been consistently deployed against government organizations, mainly in Southeast and East Asia, for cyber espionage.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Recently, this malware has resurfaced, likely targeting attendees of the 2024 <strong>International Institute for Strategic Studies</strong> (IISS) Defence Summit in Prague.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This campaign illustrates how cyber espionage and international strategy often intertwine as nations seek to infiltrate sensitive security and defense discussions to gain a strategic edge amid global conflicts, from the Russia-Ukraine war to rising tensions in the South China Sea.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">While combing through files on Hatching Triage, one name stood out, prompting us to investigate further and share our findings in this article.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This blog post will explore our findings, including the malware's execution techniques, capabilities, and the <a href="https://hunt.io/glossary/command-and-control-server-c2" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">command and control (C2) infrastructure</a> that facilitates its operations.</p>
<h2 id="The_IISS_Defence_Summit_An_Attractive_Target_for_Cyber_Espionage" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:22px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">The IISS Defence Summit: An Attractive Target for Cyber Espionage</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The IISS Prague Defence Summit, scheduled for November 8-10, 2024, is a new event modeled after the successful Shangri-La and Manama Dialogues. The summit is poised to become a central forum for discussing defense and security within the Euro-Atlantic region.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Attendees include senior political leaders, defense ministers, policymakers, and industry executives from Europe, the United States, and allied nations. Discussions include defense capacity-building, strategic stability, and emerging threats.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This summit is a prime target for cyber espionage due to the participation of high-level officials discussing sensitive issues like military strategy, defense cooperation, and responses to geopolitical tensions. Accessing these discussions offers adversaries a strategic edge by exposing major global players' defense plans and policies.</p>
<h2 id="File_Discovery_In_Triage__ANYRUN" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:22px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">File Discovery In Triage &amp; ANY.RUN</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">During routine analysis on Hatching Triage, we discovered an executable file,  <strong>"IISS PRAGUE DEFENCE SUMMIT (8 â 10 November 2024 ).exe,â</strong> uploaded on 16 August.  Given its relevance to an upcoming high-profile event, we decided to investigate further.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_1.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_1.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 1: Hatching Triage Sandbox Analysis of suspicious EXE (Source/Link: <a href="https://tria.ge/240816-pn7j9sydlj/behavioral5" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">Triage</a>)</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">To further solidify our suspicions, a review of the PCAP containing network traffic confirmed the malware communicating with its C2 server using the familiar magic bytes 17 03 03.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">These bytes often appear in posts and reports as indicators of Toneshell and PubLoad activity.<br>
<!-- -->We found the same executable file on <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/afe95446-ef52-4acb-a4fb-a76b636e8b8b/" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">ANY.RUN</a>, where it exhibited similar TTPs.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_2.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_2.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 2: ANY.RUN analysis of the IISS-themed executable. (Source/Link: <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/afe95446-ef52-4acb-a4fb-a76b636e8b8b/" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">ANY.RUN</a>)</div>
<h2 id="Decoy_Document_Analysis" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:22px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Decoy Document Analysis</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Before diving into the malware itself, letâs first examine the decoy PDF used in this attack. Upon extracting the archive, the user is presented with two folders: <strong>Annex 1</strong> and <strong>Annex 2</strong>.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The first folder contains the executable file mentioned above, while the second, contains the document seen in Figure 3 titled âAnnex 2 - IISS PRAGUE DEFENCE SUMMIT (8 â 10 November 2024) - Copy.pdf.â</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_3.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_3.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 3: Document posing as an agenda for the upcoming IISS Defence Summit</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The PDF is an exact copy of a legitimate document available on the IISS official website, with only its name altered. This tactic is designed to reassure the target by displaying a genuine agenda for the summit, reducing suspicion while the malware silently operates in the background.</p>
<h2 id="Uncovering_Malware_Behavior_and_Execution" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:22px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Uncovering Malware Behavior and Execution</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">As previously mentioned, the extracted ZIP file reveals two folders. Weâll now turn our attention to the suspicious file that caught our eye.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_4.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_4.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 4: Annex 1 &amp; 2 folders after extracting the zip contents</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Inside the Annex 1 folder (Figure 5), we see a file name matching that of what we found in Triage. For the keen-eyed, you may have noticed the file type is "Shortcut to MS-DOS Program," which suggests it is a program information file (PIF).</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_5.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_5.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 5: PIF-file masquerading as IISS agenda file</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">PIF files are shortcuts designed to provide metadata like a config file for MS-DOS programs. However, threat actors can use them as an alternative to .exe files to execute malicious code.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The PIF file acts as a dropper, which weâll soon see, and is signed by the â<strong>Hefei Nora Network Technology Co.</strong>â  A screenshot of the code signing certificate is below.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_6.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_6.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 6: Codesigning certificate used for the malicious PIF-file</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Analyzing the file in VirusTotal reveals the PIF-file has two aliases: <strong>fhbemb.exe</strong> and <strong>SFFWallpaperCore.exe</strong>.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This file also contains a PDB path of:</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><strong>G:\CLIENT\fhbemb\src\bin\Release_NL\fhbemb.pdb</strong></p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">In our research, we were unable to locate information suggesting either of the above file names (fhbemb.exe and SFFWallpaperCore.exe) are legitimate Windows programs.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">An April 2024 blog post by <a href="https://www-secrss-com.translate.goog/articles/64948?_x_tr_sl=auto&amp;_x_tr_tl=en&amp;_x_tr_hl=ja" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">secrss</a> uncovered a suspected <strong>APT-Q-27</strong> (aka Golden Eye Dog, Dragon Breath) operation that also used âfhbemb.exeâ to side load âlibemb.dllâ to execute a modified version of Gh0st RAT.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><a href="https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2023/05/03/doubled-dll-sideloading-dragon-breath/" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">Sophos</a> has also previously reported similar DLL sideloading techniques by this group.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Figure 7 illustrates the malware execution flow as detailed in the Secrss post.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_7.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_7.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 7: Secrss attack process diagram using similarly named files (Source: <a href="https://www-secrss-com.translate.goog/articles/64948?_x_tr_sl=auto&amp;_x_tr_tl=en&amp;_x_tr_hl=ja" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">Secrss</a>)</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Returning to the malicious PIF, upon execution, it checks for the presence of the FFWallpaperCore directory in C:\ProgramData. If the directory is absent, it drops SFFWallpaperCore.exe and libemb.dll, likely to verify whether the system has already been compromised.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Persistence is established by adding a registry run key and creating a scheduled task.</p>
<ul style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;margin:21px 0;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1.2em">
<li style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1em">Registry run key:</li>
</ul>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><strong>cmd.exe /C schtasks /F /Create /TN FFWallpaperEmbCore /SC minute /MO 6 /TR "C:\ProgramData\FFWallpaperCore\SFFWallpaperCore.exe FFWallpaper"</strong></p>
<ul style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;margin:21px 0;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1.2em">
<li style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-bottom:1em">Creation of scheduled task</li>
</ul>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word"><strong>schtasks /F /Create /TN FFWallpaperEmbCore /SC minute /MO 6 /TR "C:\ProgramData\FFWallpaperCore\SFFWallpaperCore.exe FFWallpaper"</strong></p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The overall execution flow (<strong>Figure 8</strong>) follows a rather standard pattern commonly seen in malware operations.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_8.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_8.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 8: PIF event flow (Created using Lucidchart)</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">libemb.dll, written in C++, is signed by the same company as the EXE, but, as shown in Figure 9, the certificate is not trusted.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_9.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_9.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 9: Untrusted codesigning certificate for libemb.dll</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The DLL contains unique debug strings, which have become a hallmark of Mustang Panda malware. Within the file, we found two references to Twitter/X accounts: @Rainmaker1973 and @techyteachme, the latter belonging to Zack Allen, who also runs a great Detection Engineering newsletter if youâre interested.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_10.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_10.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 10: Unique strings including the X account name for Zack Allen. Also notice the string before âbuitengebieden,â which is Dutch for âoutlying areas.â</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">&nbsp;</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_11.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_11.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 11: Debug strings for X user Rainmaker1973</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">A network connection is established with the C2 server at 103.27.108.]14 on port 443. The traffic uses raw TCP but mimics TLS to evade detection.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This approach has been observed in multiple reports on Mustang Panda activity, specifically linked to ToneShell and Pubload malware.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Below is a PCAP screenshot from the initial communication with the C2 server.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_12.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_12.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 12: Request header containing the magic bytes â17 03 03â</div>
<h2 id="Network_Infrastructure" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:22px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Network Infrastructure</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">The command and control server is hosted on Topway Global Limitedâs ASN in Hong Kong, with ports <strong>80</strong>, <strong>443</strong>, and <strong>3389</strong> accessible. Interestingly, the IP briefly presented a self-signed RDP certificate at the start of August, carrying the common name â<strong>WIN-USLKI5BA743</strong>.â</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Using RDP certificates has been a reliable method for tracking Mustang Pandaâs infrastructure in the past, but recent variations suggest the threat actors are aware of this detection technique and are adjusting accordingly.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">This particular certificate was issued on <strong>Wednesday, August 25, 2021, at 03:36:30</strong>âa detail that may prove significant in our investigation.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Below is a screenshot from Hunt showing this certificate, along with historical TLS data, to aid in identifying related activity.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_13.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_13.webp">
<div style="font-family:Inter;font-size:14px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:-0.5em;text-align:center;font-weight:400;margin-bottom:2em">Figure 13: SSL History data in Hunt showing the short-lived RDP certificate</div>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">With no additional domains or certificates to pivot on, we turn to Hunt's Advanced Search feature to identify servers using the same certificate, focusing specifically on the 'Not Before' date and time.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">By applying the query shown in <strong>Figure 14</strong>, we narrowed the results to just seven serversâsuggesting a potential link to the associated infrastructure. Notably, three of these servers were first observed only a few days ago, indicating recent and potentially active use at the time of writing.</p>
<img class="blog-post-image" style="display:block;margin:0 auto;max-width:90%;margin-left:1.5em;margin-right:1.5em;margin-bottom:3em;margin-inline:auto;cursor:zoom-in;border-radius:12px;overflow:hidden;box-shadow:-1px 4px 23px 0px rgba(0,0,0,0.05)" loading="lazy" src="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_14.webp" alt="https://app.hunt.io/images/blogs/toneshell/figure_14.webp">

<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">As shown in the table above, nearly all the IP addresses reside on the same ASN as the C2 server, with one exception. Additionally, the proximity of these IPs to each other strengthens our assessment that these servers may be controlled by the same threat actor or group and hosted within a similar or adjacent range to maintain operational control and flexibility.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">Notably, the C2 IP has not yet been flagged as malicious by any vendors on VirusTotal.</p>
<h2 id="Final_Thoughts" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:22px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-52fbe57f-32fe-4445-8898-2356002798ae, rgb(20, 20, 22));margin-top:1.2em;scroll-margin-top:22vh">Final Thoughts</h2>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">While sandbox runs and dynamic analysis of the malware did not reveal the specific objectives of the threat actors once they gained access to infected systems, we can hypothesize that targeting a defense summit suggests an intent to gather intelligence on sensitive discussions.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">To mitigate such threats, Hunt recommends conducting regular phishing awareness exercises for all users, closely verifying email senders and domain names before downloading files, and deploying an endpoint detection and response solution to identify malicious execution patterns.</p>
<p style="font-family:Inter;font-size:12px;line-height:1.5;color:var(--token-b1c6cb15-a9fa-43f5-85d5-1deb94f5f546, rgb(53, 57, 69));margin-top:1.2em;margin-bottom:2em;overflow-wrap:break-word">If youâd like to stay ahead of threats like those uncovered in this post, <a href="https://hunt.io/demo?utm_campaign=huntio-blog-bottom-demo-cta&amp;utm_source=hunt.io&amp;utm_medium=website&amp;utm_content=huntio-top-heading-demo-button&amp;utm_term=huntio-top-heading-demo-button" target="_blank" class="link-hover" style="font-family:Inter;font-size:16px;color:#6ea8fe">request a demo</a> today to see how our tools can enhance your defenses.</p>
